<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Ontology</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Branch of philosophy that studies concepts such as existence, being, becoming, and reality</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about ontology in philosophy. For the concept in information science and computing, see <a href="/wiki/Ontology_(information_science)" title="Ontology (information science)">Ontology (information science)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Oncology" title="Oncology">Oncology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odontology" title="Odontology">Odontology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontogeny" title="Ontogeny">Ontogeny</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Deontology" title="Deontology">Deontology</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, <b>ontology</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a>, as well as related concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Becoming_(philosophy)" title="Becoming (philosophy)">becoming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>. 
</p><p>Ontology addresses questions of how entities are grouped into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Category_of_being" title="Category of being">categories</a> and which of these entities exist on the most fundamental level. Ontologists often try to determine what the categories or highest kinds are and how they form a system of categories that encompasses classification of all entities. Commonly proposed categories include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_(philosophy)" title="Substance (philosophy)">substances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relations_(philosophy)" title="Relations (philosophy)">relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_of_affairs_(philosophy)" title="State of affairs (philosophy)">states of affairs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Event_(philosophy)" title="Event (philosophy)">events</a>. These categories are characterized by fundamental ontological concepts, including particularity and universality, abstractness and concreteness, or possibility and necessity. Of special interest is the concept of ontological dependence, which determines whether the entities of a category exist on the most fundamental level. Disagreements within ontology are often about whether entities belonging to a certain category exist and, if so, how they are related to other entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofweber_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hofweber-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>When used as a <a href="/wiki/Count_noun" title="Count noun">countable noun</a>, the words <i>ontology</i> and <i>ontologies</i> refer not to the science of being but to theories within the science of being. Ontological theories can be divided into various types according to their theoretical commitments. Monocategorical ontologies hold that there is only one basic category, but polycategorical ontologies rejected this view. Hierarchical ontologies assert that some entities exist on a more fundamental level and that other entities depend on them. Flat ontologies, on the other hand, deny such a privileged status to any entity.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Classical_compound" title="Classical compound">Classical compound</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compound</a> word <i>ontology</i> ('study of being') combines
</p>
<dl><dd><i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/onto-" title="wikt:onto-">onto</a>-</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ὄν</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">on</i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[note 1]</a></sup> <abbr title="Genitive case"><a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case"><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">GEN</span></span></a>.</abbr> <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ὄντος</span></span>, <span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">ontos</i></span>, 'being' or 'that which is') and</dd>
<dd><i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-logia" title="wikt:-logia">-logia</a></i> (<span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><span lang="en"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-%CE%BB%CE%BF%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1#English" title="wikt:-λογία">-λογία</a></span></span></span>, 'logical discourse').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ-4">[3]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> is Greek, the oldest extant records of the word itself is a <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> form <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ontologia</i></span>, which appeared
</p>
<dl><dd>in 1606 in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ogdoas_Scholastica" title="Ogdoas Scholastica">Ogdoas Scholastica</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Lorhard" title="Jacob Lorhard">Jacob Lorhard</a> (<i>Lorhardus</i>), and</dd>
<dd>in 1613 in the <i>Lexicon philosophicum</i> by <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_Goclenius" title="Rudolph Goclenius">Rudolf Göckel</a> (<i>Goclenius</i>).</dd></dl>
<p>The first occurrence in English of <i>ontology</i>, as recorded by the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> came in 1664 through <i>Archelogia philosophica nova...</i> by Gideon Harvey<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> The word was first used, in its Latin form, by philosophers, and based on the Latin roots (and in turn on the Greek ones).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ontology is closely associated with Aristotle's question of 'being <i>qua</i> being': the question of what all entities in the widest sense have in common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> The <i>Eleatic principle</i> is one answer to this question: it states that being is inextricably tied to causation, that "Power is the mark of Being".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> One problem with this answer is that it excludes abstract objects. Another explicit but little accepted answer can be found in Berkeley's slogan that "to be is to be perceived".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> Intimately related but not identical to the question of 'being <i>qua</i> being' is the problem of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Category_of_being" title="Category of being">categories</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> Categories are usually seen as the highest kinds or genera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomasson2_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomasson2-10">[9]</a></sup> A system of categories provides a classification of entities that is exclusive and exhaustive: every entity belongs to exactly one category. Various such classifications have been proposed, they often include categories for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_(philosophy)" title="Substance (philosophy)">substances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relations_(philosophy)" title="Relations (philosophy)">relations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_affairs" title="States of affairs">states of affairs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Event_(philosophy)" title="Event (philosophy)">events</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> At the core of the differentiation between categories are various fundamental ontological concepts and distinctions, for example, the concepts of <i>particularity and universality</i>, of <i>abstractness and concreteness</i>, of <i>ontological dependence</i>, of <i>identity</i> and of <i>modality</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> These concepts are sometimes treated as categories themselves, are used to explain the difference between categories or play other central roles for characterizing different ontological theories. Within ontology, there is a lack of general consensus concerning how the different categories are to be defined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomasson2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomasson2-10">[9]</a></sup> Different ontologists often disagree on whether a certain category has any members at all or whether a given category is fundamental.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particulars_and_universals">Particulars and universals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Particulars and universals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Particular" title="Particular">Particulars</a> or individuals are usually contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Universal_(metaphysics)" title="Universal (metaphysics)">universals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Universals concern features that can be exemplified by various different particulars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-14">[13]</a></sup> For example, a tomato and a strawberry are two particulars that exemplify the universal redness. Universals can be present at various distinct locations in space at the same time while particulars are restricted to one location at a time. Furthermore, universals can be fully present at different times, which is why they are sometimes referred to as <i>repeatables</i> in contrast to non-repeatable particulars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> The so-called <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a> is the problem to explain how different things can agree in their features, e.g. how a tomato and a strawberry can both be red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-14">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals#realism" title="Problem of universals">Realists</a> about universals believe that there are universals. They can solve the <i>problem of universals</i> by explaining the commonality through a universal shared by both entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Realists are divided among themselves as to whether universals can exist independently of being exemplified by something ("ante res") or not ("in rebus").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nominalism" title="Nominalism">Nominalists</a>, on the other hand, deny that there are universals. They have to resort to other notions to explain how a feature can be common to several entities, for example, by positing either fundamental resemblance-relations between the entities (resemblance nominalism) or a shared membership to a common natural class (class nominalism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abstract_and_concrete">Abstract and concrete</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Abstract and concrete">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abstract_and_concrete" title="Abstract and concrete">Abstract and concrete</a></div>
<p>Many philosophers agree that there is an exclusive and exhaustive distinction between <i>concrete objects</i> and <i>abstract objects</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Some philosophers consider this to be the most general division of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honderich_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honderich-16">[15]</a></sup> Examples of concrete objects include plants, human beings and planets while things like numbers, sets and propositions are abstract objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> But despite the general agreement concerning the paradigm cases, there is less consensus as to what the characteristic marks of concreteness and abstractness are. Popular suggestions include defining the distinction in terms of the difference between (1) existence inside or outside space-time, (2) having causes and effects or not and (3) having contingent or necessary existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontological_dependence">Ontological dependence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ontological dependence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grounding_(metaphysics)" title="Grounding (metaphysics)">Grounding (metaphysics)</a></div>
<p>An entity <i>ontologically depends</i> on another entity if the first entity cannot exist without the second entity. Ontologically independent entities, on the other hand, can exist all by themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> For example, the surface of an apple cannot exist without the apple and so depends on it ontologically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Entities often characterized as ontologically dependent include properties, which depend on their bearers, and boundaries, which depend on the entity they demarcate from its surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahko_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tahko-22">[21]</a></sup> As these examples suggest, ontological dependence is to be distinguished from causal dependence, in which an effect depends for its existence on a cause. It is often important to draw a distinction between two types of ontological dependence: rigid and generic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahko_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tahko-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Rigid dependence concerns the dependence on one specific entity, as the surface of an apple depends on its specific apple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erices_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erices-23">[22]</a></sup> Generic dependence, on the other hand, involves a weaker form of dependence, on merely a certain type of entity. For example, electricity generically depends on there being charged particles, but it does not depend on any specific charged particle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahko_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tahko-22">[21]</a></sup> Dependence-relations are relevant to ontology since it is often held that ontologically dependent entities have a less robust form of being. This way a <i>hierarchy</i> is introduced into the world that brings with it the distinction between more and less fundamental entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahko_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tahko-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">Identity</a> is a basic ontological concept that is often expressed by the word "same".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-24">[23]</a></sup> It is important to distinguish between <i>qualitative identity</i> and <i>numerical identity</i>. For example, consider two children with identical bicycles engaged in a race while their mother is watching. The two children have the <i>same</i> bicycle in one sense (<i>qualitative identity</i>) and the <i>same</i> mother in another sense (<i>numerical identity</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Two qualitatively identical things are often said to be indiscernible. The two senses of <i>sameness</i> are linked by two principles: the principle of <i>indiscernibility of identicals</i> and the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Identity_of_indiscernibles" title="Identity of indiscernibles">identity of indiscernibles</a></i>. The principle of <i>indiscernibility of identicals</i> is uncontroversial and states that if two entities are numerically identical with each other then they exactly resemble each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-24">[23]</a></sup> The principle of <i>identity of indiscernibles</i>, on the other hand, is more controversial in making the converse claim that if two entities exactly resemble each other then they must be numerically identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-24">[23]</a></sup> This entails that "no two distinct things exactly resemble each other".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> A well-known counterexample comes from <a href="/wiki/Max_Black" title="Max Black">Max Black</a>, who describes a symmetrical universe consisting of only two spheres with the same features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> Black argues that the two spheres are indiscernible but not identical, thereby constituting a violation of the principle of <i>identity of indiscernibles</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The problem of <i>identity over time</i> concerns the question of <i>persistence</i>: whether or in what sense two objects at different times can be <i>numerically identical</i>. This is usually referred to as <i>diachronic identity</i> in contrast to <i>synchronic identity</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallois_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallois-28">[27]</a></sup> The statement that "[t]he table in the next room is identical with the one you purchased last year" asserts diachronic identity between the table now and the table then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallois_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gallois-28">[27]</a></sup> A famous example of a denial of diachronic identity comes from <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a>, who argues that it is impossible to step into the same river twice because of the changes that occurred in-between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-29">[28]</a></sup> The traditional position on the problem of <i>persistence</i> is <a href="/wiki/Endurantism" title="Endurantism">endurantism</a>, the thesis that diachronic identity in a strict sense is possible. One problem with this position is that it seems to violate the principle of <i>indiscernibility of identicals</i>: the object may have undergone changes in the meantime resulting in it being discernible from itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Perdurantism" title="Perdurantism">Perdurantism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Four-dimensionalism" title="Four-dimensionalism">four-dimensionalism</a> is an alternative approach holding that <i>diachronic identity</i> is possible only in a loose sense: while the two objects differ from each other strictly speaking, they are both temporal parts that belong to the same temporally extended whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-11"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> <i>Perdurantism</i> avoids many philosophical problems plaguing <i>endurantism</i>, but <i>endurantism</i> seems to be more in touch with how we ordinarily conceive <i>diachronic identity</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallois_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gallois-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modality">Modality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Modality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">Modality</a> concerns the concepts of possibility, actuality and necessity. In contemporary discourse, these concepts are often defined in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possible_worlds" title="Possible worlds">possible worlds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-12"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> A possible world is a complete way how things could have been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> The actual world is one possible world among others: things could have been different from what they actually are. A proposition is possibly true if there is at least one possible world in which it is true; it is necessarily true if it is true in all possible worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Actualism" title="Actualism">Actualists</a></i> and <i>possibilists</i> disagree on the <i>ontological status</i> of possible worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-13"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Actualists hold that reality is at its core actual and that possible worlds should be understood in terms of actual entities, for example, as fictions or as sets of sentences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parent_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parent-33">[32]</a></sup> Possibilists, on the other hand, assign to possible worlds the same <i>fundamental ontological status</i> as to the actual world. This is a form of <a href="/wiki/Modal_realism" title="Modal realism">modal realism</a>, holding that reality has <i>irreducibly modal features</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parent_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parent-33">[32]</a></sup> Another important issue in this field concerns the distinction between <i>contingent</i> and <i>necessary beings</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-14"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Contingent beings are beings whose existence is possible but not necessary. Necessary beings, on the other hand, could not have failed to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> It has been suggested that this distinction is the highest division of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-15"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Substances">Substances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Substances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The category of <i>substances</i> has played a central role in many ontological theories throughout the history of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kim_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-kim-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-38">[37]</a></sup> "Substance" is a technical term within philosophy not to be confused with the more common usage in the sense of chemical substances like gold or sulfur. Various definitions have been given but among the most common features ascribed to substances in the philosophical sense is that they are <i>particulars</i> that are <i>ontologically independent</i>: they are able to exist all by themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kim_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-kim-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> Being ontologically independent, substances can play the role of <i>fundamental entities</i> in the <i>ontological hierarchy</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahko_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tahko-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-38">[37]</a></sup> If 'ontological independence' is defined as including <i>causal independence</i> then only self-caused entities, like Spinoza's God, can be substances. With a specifically ontological definition of 'independence', many everyday objects like books or cats may qualify as substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kim_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-kim-37">[36]</a></sup> Another defining feature often attributed to substances is their ability to <i>undergo changes</i>. Changes involve something existing <i>before</i>, <i>during</i> and <i>after</i> the change. They can be described in terms of a persisting substance gaining or losing properties, or of <i>matter</i> changing its <i>form</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kim_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-kim-37">[36]</a></sup> From this perspective, the ripening of a tomato may be described as a change in which the tomato loses its greenness and gains its redness. It is sometimes held that a substance can have a property in two ways: <i><a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essentially</a></i> and <i>accidentally</i>. A substance can survive a change of <i>accidental properties</i> but it cannot lose its <i>essential properties</i>, which constitute its nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robertson_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_relations">Properties and relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Properties and relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">Property (philosophy)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Relations_(philosophy)" title="Relations (philosophy)">Relations (philosophy)</a></div>
<p>The category of <i>properties</i> consists of entities that can be exemplified by other entities, e.g. by substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orilia_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orilia-40">[39]</a></sup> Properties characterize their bearers, they express what their bearer is like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> For example, the red color and the round shape of an apple are properties of this apple. Various ways have been suggested concerning how to conceive properties themselves and their relation to substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-16"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> The traditionally dominant view is that properties are universals that inhere in their bearers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> As universals, they can be shared by different substances. Nominalists, on the other hand, deny that universals exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-14">[13]</a></sup> Some nominalists try to account for properties in terms of resemblance relations or class membership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-17"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Another alternative for nominalists is to conceptualize properties as simple particulars, so-called <a href="/wiki/Trope_(philosophy)#In_metaphysics" title="Trope (philosophy)">tropes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> This position entails that both the apple and its redness are particulars. Different apples may still exactly resemble each other concerning their color, but they do not share the same particular property on this view: the two color-tropes are <i>numerically distinct</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacLeod_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-MacLeod-14">[13]</a></sup> Another important question for any theory of properties is how to conceive the relation between a bearer and its properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-18"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> Substratum theorists hold that there is some kind of substance, <i>substratum</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Substance_theory#Bare_particular" title="Substance theory">bare particular</a></i> that acts as bearer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benovsky_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benovsky-41">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bundle_theory" title="Bundle theory">Bundle theory</a> is an alternative view that does away with a substratum altogether: objects are taken to be just a bundle of properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> They are held together not by a substratum but by the so-called <i>compresence-relation</i> responsible for the bundling. Both substratum theory and bundle theory can be combined with conceptualizing properties as universals or as particulars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benovsky_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Benovsky-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important distinction among properties is between <i>categorical</i> and <i>dispositional</i> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kriegel_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kriegel-43">[42]</a></sup> Categorical properties concern what something is like, e.g. what qualities it has. Dispositional properties, on the other hand, involve what powers something has, what it is able to do, even if it is not actually doing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup> For example, the shape of a sugar cube is a categorical property while its tendency to dissolve in water is a dispositional property. For many properties there is a lack of consensus as to how they should be classified, for example, whether colors are categorical or dispositional properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)#Categoricalism_vs._dispositionalism" title="Property (philosophy)">Categoricalism</a> is the thesis that on a fundamental level there are only categorical properties, that dispositional properties are either non-existent or dependent on categorical properties. <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)#Categoricalism_vs._dispositionalism" title="Property (philosophy)">Dispositionalism</a> is the opposite theory, giving ontological primacy to dispositional properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Choi-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kriegel_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kriegel-43">[42]</a></sup> Between these two extremes, there are dualists who allow both categorical and dispositional properties in their ontology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orilia_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Orilia-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Relations</i> are ways in which things, the relata, stand to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Relations are in many ways similar to properties in that both characterize the things they apply to. Properties are sometimes treated as a special case of relations involving only one relatum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orilia_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Orilia-40">[39]</a></sup> Central for ontology is the distinction between <i>internal</i> and <i>external</i> relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacBride_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacBride-47">[46]</a></sup> A relation is <i>internal</i> if it is fully determined by the features of its relata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> For example, an apple and a tomato stand in the <i>internal relation</i> of <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(philosophy)" title="Similarity (philosophy)">similarity</a> to each other because they are both red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Some philosophers have inferred from this that internal relations do not have a proper ontological status since they can be reduced to intrinsic properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacBride_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacBride-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> <i>External</i> relations, on the other hand, are not fixed by the features of their relata. For example, a book stands in an <i>external</i> relation to a table by lying on top of it. But this is not determined by the book's or the table's features like their color, their shape, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacBride_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacBride-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_of_affairs_and_events">States of affairs and events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: States of affairs and events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/State_of_affairs_(philosophy)" title="State of affairs (philosophy)">States of affairs</a> are complex entities, in contrast to substances and properties, which are usually conceived as simple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-14"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Complex entities are built up from or constituted by other entities. Atomic states of affairs are constituted by one particular and one property exemplified by this particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-19"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Textor_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Textor-52">[51]</a></sup> For example, the state of affairs that Socrates is wise is constituted by the particular "Socrates" and the property "wise". Relational states of affairs involve several particulars and a relation connecting them. States of affairs that <i>obtain</i> are also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Fact#In_philosophy" title="Fact">facts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Textor_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Textor-52">[51]</a></sup> It is controversial which ontological status should be ascribed to states of affairs that do not obtain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler2_11-20"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler2-11">[10]</a></sup> States of affairs have been prominent in 20th-century ontology as various theories were proposed to describe the world as composed of states of affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert2_7-15"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert2-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> It is often held that states of affairs play the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truthmaker" title="Truthmaker">truthmakers</a>: judgments or assertions are true because the corresponding state of affairs obtains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Textor_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Textor-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Event_(philosophy)" title="Event (philosophy)">Events</a> take place in time, they are sometimes thought of as involving a change in the form of acquiring or losing a property, like the lawn's becoming dry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honderich2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honderich2-56">[55]</a></sup> But on a liberal view, the retaining of a property without any change may also count as an event, e.g. the lawn's staying wet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honderich2_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Honderich2-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2-57">[56]</a></sup> Some philosophers see events as universals that can repeat at different times, but the more dominant view is that events are particulars and therefore non-repeatable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2-57">[56]</a></sup> Some events are complex in that they are composed of a sequence of events, often referred to as a process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> But even simple events can be conceived as complex entities involving an object, a time and the property exemplified by the object at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> So-called <a href="/wiki/Process_philosophy" title="Process philosophy">process philosophy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Process_ontology" title="Process ontology">process ontology</a> ascribes ontological primacy to changes and processes as opposed to the emphasis on static being in the traditionally dominant substance metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reality_of_things">Reality of things</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reality of things">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word 'real' is derived from the Latin word <i>res</i>, which is often translated as 'thing'. The word 'thing' is often used in ontological discourse as if it had a presupposed meaning, not needing an explicit philosophical definition because it belongs to ordinary language. Nevertheless, what is a thing and what is real or substantial are concerns of ontology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erices_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erices-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isham_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isham-66">[65]</a></sup> Different views are held about this. Plato proposed that underlying, and constituting the real basis of, the concretely experienced world are '<a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">forms</a>' or 'ideas', which today are generally regarded as high abstractions. In earlier days, philosophers used the term '<a href="/wiki/Platonic_realism" title="Platonic realism">realism</a>' to refer to Plato's belief that his 'forms' are 'real'; nowadays, the term 'realism' often has an almost opposite meaning, so that Plato's belief is sometimes called '<a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Philosophers debate whether entities such as tables and chairs, lions and tigers, philosophical doctrines, numbers, truth, and beauty, are to be regarded as 'things', or as something or nothing 'real'.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_ontologies">Types of ontologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Types of ontologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ontological theories can be divided into various types according to their theoretical commitments. Particular ontological theories or types of theories are often referred to as "ontologies" (<a href="/wiki/Count_noun" title="Count noun">singular or plural</a>). This usage contrasts with the meaning of "ontology" (<a href="/wiki/Mass_noun" title="Mass noun">only singular</a>) as a branch of philosophy: the <i>science of being</i> in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flat_vs_polycategorical_vs_hierarchical">Flat vs polycategorical vs hierarchical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Flat vs polycategorical vs hierarchical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One way to divide ontologies is by the number of basic categories they use. <i>Monocategorical</i> or <i>one category ontologies</i> hold that there is only one basic category while <i>polycategorical ontologies</i> imply that there are several distinct basic categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inwagen_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inwagen-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Another way to divide ontologies is through the notion of ontological hierarchy. Hierarchical ontologies assert that some entities exist on a more fundamental level and that other entities depend on them. Flat ontologies, on the other hand, deny such a privileged status to any entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> Jonathan Schaffer provides an overview of these positions by distinguishing between <i>flat</i> ontologies (non-hierarchical), <i>sorted</i> ontologies (polycategorical non-hierarchical) and <i>ordered</i> ontologies (polycategorical hierarchical).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffer_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Flat</i> ontologies are only interested in the difference between existence and non-existence. They are <i>flat</i> because each flat ontology can be represented by a simple set containing all the entities to which this ontology is committed. An influential exposition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> of this approach comes from <a href="/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine" title="Willard Van Orman Quine">Willard Van Orman Quine</a> which is why it has been referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Meta-ontology#Quinean_approach" title="Meta-ontology">Quinean</a> approach to <a href="/wiki/Meta-ontology" title="Meta-ontology">meta-ontology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffer_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> This outlook does not deny that the existing entities can be further subdivided and may stand in various relations to each other. These issues are questions for the more specific sciences, but they do not belong to ontology in the Quinean sense.
</p><p><i>Polycategorical</i> ontologies are concerned with the categories of being. Each polycategorical ontology posits a number of categories. These categories are exclusive and exhaustive: every existing entity belongs to exactly one category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffer_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-75">[74]</a></sup> A recent example of a polycategorical ontology is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.J._Lowe" title="E.J. Lowe">E.J. Lowe</a>'s four-category-ontology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-78">[77]</a></sup> The four categories are object, kind, mode and attribute. The fourfold structure is based on two distinctions. The first distinction is between substantial entities (objects and kinds) and non-substantial entities (modes and attributes). The second distinction is between particular entities (objects and modes) and universal entities (kinds and attributes). Reality is built up through the interplay of entities belonging to different categories: particular entities instantiate universal entities, and non-substantial entities characterize substantial entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Hierarchical</i> ontologies are interested in the degree of fundamentality of the entities they posit. Their main goal is to figure out which entities are fundamental and how the non-fundamental entities depend on them. The concept of fundamentality is usually defined in terms of <a href="/wiki/Grounding_(metaphysics)" title="Grounding (metaphysics)">metaphysical grounding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Fundamental entities are different from non-fundamental entities because they are not grounded in other entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffer_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffer-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> For example, it is sometimes held that elementary particles are more fundamental than the macroscopic objects (like chairs and tables) they compose. This is a claim about the grounding-relation between microscopic and macroscopic objects. Schaffer's priority monism is a recent form of a hierarchical ontology. He holds that on the most fundamental level there exists only one thing: the world as a whole. This thesis does not deny our common-sense intuition that the distinct objects we encounter in our everyday affairs like cars or other people exist. It only denies that these objects have the most fundamental form of existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> An example of a <i>hierarchical</i> ontology in <a href="/wiki/Continental_philosophy" title="Continental philosophy">continental philosophy</a> comes from <a href="/wiki/Nicolai_Hartmann" title="Nicolai Hartmann">Nicolai Hartmann</a>. He asserts that reality is made up of four <a href="/wiki/Integrative_level" title="Integrative level">levels</a>: the inanimate, the biological, the psychological and the spiritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poli_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poli-83">[82]</a></sup> These levels form a hierarchy in the sense that the higher levels depend on the lower levels while the lower levels are indifferent to the higher levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartmann_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thing_ontologies_vs_fact_ontologies">Thing ontologies vs fact ontologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Thing ontologies vs fact ontologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Thing ontologies</i> and <i>fact ontologies</i> are one-category-ontologies: they both hold that all fundamental entities belong to the same category. They disagree on whether this category is the category of things or of facts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> A slogan for fact ontologies comes from <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a>: "The world is the totality of facts, not of things".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>One difficulty in characterizing this dispute is to elucidate what things and facts are, and how they differ from each other. Things are commonly contrasted with the properties and relations they instantiate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-89">[88]</a></sup> Facts, on the other hand, are often characterized as having these things and the properties/relations as their constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armstrong_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-90">[89]</a></sup> This is reflected in a rough linguistic characterization of this difference where the subjects and objects of an assertion refer to things while the assertion as a whole refers to a fact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reism is one form of thing ontology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woleński_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woleński-92">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Franz_Brentano" title="Franz Brentano">Franz Brentano</a> developed a version of reism in his later philosophy. He held that only concrete particular things exist. Things can exist in two forms: either as spatio-temporal bodies or as temporal souls. Brentano was aware of the fact that many common-sense expressions seem to refer to entities that do not have a place in his ontology, like properties or intentional objects. This is why he developed a method to paraphrase these expressions in order to avoid these ontological commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woleński_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Woleński-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D._M._Armstrong" title="D. M. Armstrong">D. M. Armstrong</a> is a well-known defender of fact ontology. He and his followers refer to facts as states of affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armstrong_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-90">[89]</a></sup> States of affairs are the basic building blocks of his ontology: they have particulars and universals as their constituents but they are primary in relation to particulars and universals. States of affairs have ontologically independent existence while "[u]npropertied particulars and uninstantiated universals are false abstractions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armstrong_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constituent_ontologies_vs_blob_theories">Constituent ontologies vs blob theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Constituent ontologies vs blob theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Constituent ontologies</i> and <i>blob theories</i>, sometimes referred to as <i>relational ontologies</i>, are concerned with the internal structure of objects. Constituent ontologies hold that objects have an internal structure made up of constituents. This is denied by blob theories: they contend that objects are structureless "blobs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inwagen_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Inwagen-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bundle_theory" title="Bundle theory">Bundle theories</a> are examples of constituent ontologies. Bundle theorists assert that an object is nothing but the properties it "has". On this account, a regular apple could be characterized as a bundle of redness, roundness, sweetness, etc. Defenders of bundle theory disagree on the nature of the bundled properties. Some affirm that these properties are universals while others contend that they are particulars, so-called "tropes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Class <a href="/wiki/Nominalism" title="Nominalism">nominalism</a>, on the other hand, is a form of blob theory. Class nominalists hold that properties are classes of things. To instantiate a property is merely to be a member of the corresponding class. So properties are not constituents of the objects that have them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Information_science_and_natural_sciences">Information science and natural sciences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Information science and natural sciences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ontology_(information_science)" title="Ontology (information science)">information science</a> ontologies are classified in various ways, using criteria such as the degree of abstraction and field of application:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petrov_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petrov-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><i><a href="/wiki/Upper_ontology" title="Upper ontology">Upper ontology</a></i>: concepts supporting development of an ontology, <a href="/wiki/Meta-ontology" title="Meta-ontology">meta-ontology</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ontology_(information_science)" title="Ontology (information science)">Domain ontology</a></i>: concepts relevant to a particular topic, <a href="/wiki/Domain_of_discourse" title="Domain of discourse">domain of discourse</a>, or area of interest, for example, to information technology or to computer languages, or to particular branches of science.</li>
<li><i>Interface ontology</i>: concepts relevant to the juncture of two disciplines.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Process_ontology" title="Process ontology">Process ontology</a></i>: inputs, outputs, constraints, sequencing information, involved in business or engineering processes.</li></ol>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Biomedical_sciences" title="Biomedical sciences">biomedical sciences</a>, ontologies have been used to create terminologies for various aspects of living organism or medical applications. A prominent example is the <a href="/wiki/Gene_Ontology" title="Gene Ontology">gene ontology</a>, but many other ontologies exist, e.g. for anatomical terms or physiology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">[97]</a></sup> Standards have been established to maintain and organize biological ontologies under the <a href="/wiki/OBO_Foundry" title="OBO Foundry">OBO</a> (Open Biological Ontologies) project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greek">Ancient Greek</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ancient Greek">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parmenides.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="283" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Parmenides.jpg/200px-Parmenides.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Parmenides.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parmenides.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> was among the first to propose an ontological characterization of the fundamental nature of reality.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Ontology%22" rel="nofollow">"Ontology"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Ontology%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Ontology%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Ontology%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Ontology%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Ontology%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek philosophical tradition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> was among the first to propose an ontological characterization of the fundamental nature of existence. In the prologue (or <a href="/wiki/Parmenides#Proem" title="Parmenides"><i>proem</i></a>) to <i>On Nature</i>, he describes two views of <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>. Initially, nothing comes from nothing, thus <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a> is <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">eternal</a>. This posits that existence is what may be conceived of by thought, created, or possessed. Hence, there may be neither void nor vacuum; and true reality neither may come into being nor vanish from existence. Rather, the entirety of creation is eternal, uniform, and immutable, though not infinite (Parmenides characterized its shape as that of a perfect sphere). Parmenides thus posits that change, as perceived in everyday experience, is illusory.
</p><p>Opposite to the <a href="/wiki/Eleatics" title="Eleatics">Eleatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(philosophy)" title="Pluralism (philosophy)">pluralistic</a> conception of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a>. In the 5th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leucippus" title="Leucippus">Leucippus</a> replaced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> the reality of <i>being</i> (unique and unchanging) with that of <a href="/wiki/Becoming_(philosophy)" title="Becoming (philosophy)"><i>becoming</i></a>, therefore by a more fundamental and elementary <i><a href="/wiki/Ontic" title="Ontic">ontic</a></i> plurality. This thesis originated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenic_world" title="Hellenic world">Hellenic world</a>, stated in two different ways by Anaxagoras and by Leucippus. The first theory dealt with "seeds" (which Aristotle referred to as "<i>homeomeries</i>") of the various substances. The second was the <a href="/wiki/Atom_(order_theory)" title="Atom (order theory)">atomistic</a> theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> which dealt with reality as based on the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>, the atoms and their intrinsic movement in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>The materialist <i><a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a></i> proposed by Leucippus was <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">indeterminist</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 460 – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 370 BC) subsequently developed it in a <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">deterministic</a> way. Later (4th century BC), <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> took the original <i>atomism</i> again as indeterministic. He saw reality as composed of an infinity of indivisible, unchangeable corpuscles or <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> (from the Greek <i>atomon</i>, lit. 'uncuttable'), but he gives weight to characterize atoms whereas for Leucippus they are characterized by a "figure", an "order" and a "position" in the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Atoms are, besides, creating the whole with the intrinsic movement in the <i>vacuum</i>, producing the diverse flux of being. Their movement is influenced by the <i>parenklisis</i> (<a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a> names it <i><a href="/wiki/Clinamen" title="Clinamen">clinamen</a></i>) and that is determined by <a href="/wiki/Randomness" title="Randomness">chance</a>. These ideas foreshadowed the understanding of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">traditional physics</a> until the advent of 20th-century theories on the nature of atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plato">Plato</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Plato">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> developed the distinction between true reality and illusion, in arguing that what is real are eternal and unchanging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">forms</a> or ideas (a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Universal_(metaphysics)" title="Universal (metaphysics)"><i>universals</i></a>), of which things experienced in sensation are at best merely copies, and real only in so far as they copy ("partake of") such forms. In general, Plato presumes that all nouns (e.g., "beauty") refer to real entities, whether sensible bodies or insensible forms. Hence, in <a href="/wiki/Sophist_(dialogue)" title="Sophist (dialogue)"><i>The Sophist</i></a>, Plato argues that <i>being</i> is a <i>form</i> in which all existent things participate and which they have in common (though it is unclear whether "Being" is intended in the sense of <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">identity</a>); and argues, against <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>, that forms must exist not only of <i>being</i>, but also of <a href="/wiki/Negation" title="Negation">Negation</a> and of non-being (or <i>Difference</i>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotle">Aristotle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Aristotle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In his <a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)" title="Categories (Aristotle)"><i>Categories</i></a>, Aristotle (384–322 BCE) identifies ten possible kinds of things that may be the subject or the predicate of a proposition. For Aristotle there are four different ontological dimensions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>according to the various categories or ways of addressing a being as such</li>
<li>according to its truth or falsity (e.g. fake gold, counterfeit money)</li>
<li>whether it exists in and of itself or simply 'comes along' by accident</li>
<li>according to its potency, movement (energy) or finished presence (<a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)"><i>Metaphysics</i> Book Theta</a>).</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindu_philosophy">Hindu philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hindu philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ontology features in the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a> from the first millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Samkhya philosophy regards the universe as consisting of two independent realities: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puru%E1%B9%A3a" title="Puruṣa">puruṣa</a> (pure, contentless consciousness) and <a href="/wiki/Prak%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Prakṛti">prakṛti</a> (matter). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_dualism" title="Substance dualism">substance dualism</a> between puruṣa and prakṛti is similar but not identical to the substance dualism between mind and body that, following the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a>, has been central to many disputes in the Western philosophical tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweizer_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schweizer-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 845">: 845 </span></sup> Samkhya sees the mind as being the subtle part of prakṛti. It is made up of three faculties: the sense mind (manas), the intellect (<a href="/wiki/Buddhi" title="Buddhi">buddhi</a>), and the ego (<a href="/wiki/Ahamkara" title="Ahamkara">ahaṁkāra</a>). These faculties perform various functions but are by themselves unable to produce consciousness, which belongs to a distinct ontological category and for which puruṣa alone is responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schweizer_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schweizer-106">[105]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">Yoga school</a> agrees with Samkhya philosophy on the fundamental dualism between puruṣa and prakṛti but it differs from Samkhya's atheistic position by incorporating the concept of a "personal, yet essentially inactive, deity" or "personal god" (<a href="/wiki/Ishvara" title="Ishvara">Ishvara</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpfl_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-lpfl-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> These two schools stand in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>, which adheres to non-duality by revealing that the apparent plurality of things is an illusion (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_(Hinduism)" title="Maya (Hinduism)">Maya</a>) hiding the true oneness of reality at its most fundamental level (<a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Medieval">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Medieval ontology</i> was strongly influenced by Aristotle's teachings. The thinkers of this period often relied on Aristotelian categories like <i>substance</i>, <i>act and potency</i> or <i>matter and form</i> to formulate their own theories. Important ontologists in this epoch include Avicenna, Thomas Aquinas, <a href="/wiki/Duns_Scotus" title="Duns Scotus">Duns Scotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Avicenna">Avicenna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Avicenna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>'s interpretation of Greek Aristotelian and Platonist ontological doctrines in medieval <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, being is either necessary, contingent <i>qua</i> possible, or impossible. Necessary being is that which cannot but be, since its non-being would entail a contradiction. Contingent <i>qua</i> possible being is neither necessary nor impossible for it to be or not to be. It is ontologically neutral, and is brought from potential existing into actual existence by way of a cause that is external to its essence. Its being is borrowed – unlike the necessary existent, which is self-subsisting and impossible not to be. As for the impossible, it necessarily does not exist, and the affirmation of its being would involve a contradiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquinas">Aquinas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Aquinas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
