<p>Fundamental to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>'s ontology is his distinction between <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">existence</a>: all entities are conceived as composites of essence and existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> The essence of a thing is what this thing is like, it signifies the definition of this thing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> God has a special status since He is the only entity whose essence is identical to its existence. But for all other, finite entities there is a <i>real distinction</i> between essence and existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> This distinction shows itself, for example, in our ability to understand the essence of something without knowing about its existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> Aquinas conceives of existence as an <i>act of being</i> that actualizes the potency given by the essence. Different things have different essences, which impose different limits on the corresponding <i>act of being</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr-118">[117]</a></sup> The paradigm examples of essence-existence-composites are material substances like cats or trees. Aquinas incorporates Aristotle's distinction between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matter_and_form" title="Matter and form">matter and form</a> by holding that the essence of <i>material</i> things, as opposed to the essence of <i>immaterial</i> things like angels, is the composition of their matter and form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> So, for example, the essence of a marble statue would be the composition of the marble (its matter) and the shape it has (its form). Form is universal since substances made of different matter can have the same form. The forms of a substance may be divided into substantial and accidental forms. A substance can survive a change of an accidental form but ceases to exist upon a change of a substantial form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kerr_118-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kerr-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ontology is increasingly seen as a separate domain of philosophy in the <i>modern period</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> Many ontological theories of this period were rationalistic in the sense that they saw ontology largely as a deductive discipline that starts from a small set of first principles or axioms, a position best exemplified by Baruch Spinoza and Christian Wolff. This rationalism in metaphysics and ontology was strongly opposed by <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>, who insisted that many claims arrived at this way are to be dismissed since they go beyond any possible experience that could justify them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Descartes">Descartes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Descartes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>' ontological distinction between mind and body has been one of the most influential parts of his philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skirry_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skirry-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup> On his view, minds are thinking things while bodies are extended things. <i>Thought</i> and <i>extension</i> are two <i>attributes</i> that each come in various <i>modes</i> of being. Modes of <i>thinking</i> include judgments, doubts, volitions, sensations and emotions while the shapes of material things are modes of <i>extension</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-129">[128]</a></sup> Modes come with a lower degree of reality since they depend for their existence on a substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> Substances, on the other hand, can exist on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-129">[128]</a></sup> Descartes' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substance_dualism" title="Substance dualism">substance dualism</a> asserts that every finite substance is either a thinking substance or an extended substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> This position does not entail that minds and bodies <i>actually are</i> separated from each other, which would defy the intuition that we both have a body and a mind. Instead, it implies that minds and bodies <i>can</i>, at least in principle, be separated, since they are distinct substances and therefore are capable of independent existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skirry_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Skirry-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> A longstanding problem for substance dualism since its inception has been to explain how minds and bodies can <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_mental_causation" title="Problem of mental causation">causally interact</a> with each other, as they apparently do, when a volition causes an arm to move or when light falling on the retina causes a visual impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skirry_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Skirry-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spinoza">Spinoza</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Spinoza">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> is well-known for his <i>substance monism:</i> the thesis that only one substance exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dutton_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dutton-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup> He refers to this substance as "God or Nature", emphasizing both his <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> This substance has an infinite amount of attributes, which he defines as "what the intellect perceives of substance as constituting its essence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> Of these attributes, only two are accessible to the human mind: thought and extension. <i>Modes</i> are properties of a substance that follow from its attributes and therefore have only a dependent form of existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> Spinoza sees everyday-things like rocks, cats or ourselves as mere modes and thereby opposes the traditional <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Cartesian</a> conception of categorizing them as substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waller_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waller-138">[137]</a></sup> Modes compose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deterministic" title="Deterministic">deterministic</a> systems in which the different modes are linked to each other as cause and effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dutton_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dutton-134">[133]</a></sup> Each deterministic system corresponds to one attribute: one for extended things, one for thinking things, etc. Causal relations only happen within a system while the different systems run in parallel without causally interacting with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waller_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Waller-138">[137]</a></sup> Spinoza calls the system of modes <i>Natura naturata</i> ("nature natured") and opposes it to <i>Natura naturans</i> ("nature naturing"), the attributes responsible for the modes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> Everything in Spinoza's system is necessary: there are no contingent entities. This is so since the attributes are themselves necessary and since the system of modes follows from them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dutton_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dutton-134">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wolff">Wolff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Wolff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher)" title="Christian Wolff (philosopher)">Christian Wolff</a> defines ontology as the science of being in general. He sees it as a part of metaphysics besides cosmology, psychology and natural theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hettche_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hettche-141">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> According to Wolff, it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deductive" title="Deductive">deductive</a> science, knowable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_priori" title="A priori">a priori</a> and based on two fundamental principles: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principle_of_non-contradiction" title="Principle of non-contradiction">principle of non-contradiction</a> ("it cannot happen that the same thing is and is not") and the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_sufficient_reason" title="Principle of sufficient reason">principle of sufficient reason</a> ("nothing exists without a sufficient reason for why it exists rather than does not exist").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup> <i>Beings</i> are defined by their <i>determinations</i> or <i>predicates</i>, which cannot involve a contradiction. Determinates come in 3 types: <i>essentialia</i>, <i>attributes</i>, and <i>modes</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-140">[139]</a></sup> <i>Essentialia</i> define the nature of a being and are therefore necessary properties of this being. <i>Attributes</i> are determinations that follow from essentialia and are equally necessary, in contrast to <i>modes</i>, which are merely contingent. Wolff conceives <i>existence</i> as just one determination among others, which a being may lack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hettche_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hettche-141">[140]</a></sup> Ontology is interested in being at large, not just in actual being. But all beings, whether actually existing or not, have a sufficient reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-126">[125]</a></sup> The sufficient reason for things without actual existence consists in all the determinations that make up the essential nature of this thing. Wolff refers to this as a "reason of being" and contrasts it with a "reason of becoming", which explains why some things have actual existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hettche_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hettche-141">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Schopenhauer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> was a proponent of <a href="/wiki/Voluntarism_(philosophy)#Metaphysical_voluntarism" title="Voluntarism (philosophy)">metaphysical voluntarism</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> he regards will as the underlying and ultimate reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> Reality as a whole consists only of one will, which is equated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kantian" title="Kantian">Kantian</a> <a href="/wiki/Thing-in-itself" title="Thing-in-itself">thing-in-itself</a>. Like the Kantian thing-in-itself, the will exists outside space and time. But, unlike the Kantian thing-in-itself, the will has an experiential component to it: it comes in the form of striving, desiring, feeling, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> The manifold of things we encounter in our everyday experiences, like trees or cars, are mere appearances that lack existence independent of the observer. Schopenhauer describes them as objectivations of the will. These objectivations happen in different "steps", which correspond to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_forms" title="Platonic forms">platonic forms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> All objectivations are grounded in the will. This grounding is governed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principium_individuationis" title="Principium individuationis">principium individuationis</a>, which enables a manifold of individual things spread out in space and time to be grounded in the one will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kastrup_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kastrup-148">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dominant approaches to ontology in the <i>20th century</i> were phenomenology, linguistic analysis and naturalism. <i>Phenomenological ontology</i>, as exemplified by Edmund Husserl and Martin Heidegger, relies for its method on the description of experience. <i>Linguistic analysis</i> assigns to language a central role for ontology, as seen, for example, in Rudolf Carnap's thesis that the truth value of existence-claims depends on the linguistic framework in which they are made. <i>Naturalism</i> gives a prominent position to the natural sciences for the purpose of finding and evaluating ontological claims. This position is exemplified by Quine's method of ontology, which involves analyzing the ontological commitments of scientific theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Husserl">Husserl</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Husserl">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edmund_Husserl" title="Edmund Husserl">Edmund Husserl</a> sees ontology as a <i>science of essences</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup> <i>Sciences of essences</i> are contrasted with <i>factual sciences</i>: the former are knowable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_priori" title="A priori">a priori</a> and provide the foundation for the later, which are knowable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_posteriori" title="A posteriori">a posteriori</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gander_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gander-149">[148]</a></sup> Ontology as a science of essences is not interested in <i>actual facts</i>, but in the essences themselves, whether they <i>have instances or not</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> Husserl distinguishes between <i>formal ontology</i>, which investigates the essence of <i>objectivity in general</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> and <i>regional ontologies</i>, which study <i>regional essences</i> that are shared by all entities belonging to the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup> Regions correspond to the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a> of <a href="/wiki/Abstract_and_concrete" title="Abstract and concrete">concrete entities</a>: material nature, personal consciousness and interpersonal spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup> Husserl's method for studying ontology and sciences of essence in general is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eidetic_variation" title="Eidetic variation">eidetic variation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gander_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gander-149">[148]</a></sup> It involves imagining an object of the kind under investigation and varying its features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> The changed feature is <i>inessential</i> to this kind if the object can survive its change, otherwise it belongs to the <i>kind's essence</i>. For example, a triangle remains a triangle if one of its sides is extended but it ceases to be a triangle if a fourth side is added. Regional ontology involves applying this method to the essences corresponding to the highest genera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heidegger">Heidegger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Heidegger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Central to <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>'s philosophy is the notion of <i>ontological difference</i>: the difference between <i>being</i> as such and specific entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-156">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> He accuses the philosophical tradition of being <i>forgetful</i> of this distinction, which has led to the mistake of understanding <i>being</i> as such as a kind of ultimate entity, for example as "idea, energeia, substance, monad or will to power".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-156">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-5"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korab-Karpowicz_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korab-Karpowicz-158">[157]</a></sup> Heidegger tries to rectify this mistake in his own "fundamental ontology" by focusing on the <i>meaning of being</i> instead, a project which is akin to contemporary <a href="/wiki/Meta-ontology" title="Meta-ontology">meta-ontology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> One method to achieve this is by studying the human being, or <a href="/wiki/Dasein" title="Dasein">Dasein</a>, in Heidegger's terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup> The reason for this is that we already have a <i>pre-ontological understanding</i> of <i>being</i> that shapes how we experience the world. <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">Phenomenology</a> can be used to make this implicit understanding explicit, but it has to be accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics#Heidegger_(1889–1976)" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a> in order to avoid the distortions due to the <i>forgetfulness of being</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler_156-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-156">[155]</a></sup> In his later philosophy, Heidegger attempted to reconstruct the "history of being" in order to show how the different epochs in the history of philosophy were dominated by different conceptions of <i>being</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> His goal is to retrieve the <i>original experience of being</i> present in the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">early Greek thought</a> that was covered up by later philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korab-Karpowicz_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Korab-Karpowicz-158">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hartmann">Hartmann</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Hartmann">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nicolai_Hartmann" title="Nicolai Hartmann">Nicolai Hartmann</a> is a 20th-century philosopher within the <a href="/wiki/Continental_philosophy" title="Continental philosophy">continental tradition of philosophy</a>. He interprets ontology as <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s science of being qua being: the science of the most general characteristics of entities, usually referred to as categories, and the relations between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> According to Hartmann, the most general categories are <i>moments of being</i> (existence and essence), <i>modes of being</i> (reality and ideality) and <i>modalities of being</i> (possibility, actuality and necessity). Every entity has both <i>existence</i> and <i>essence</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> <i>Reality</i> and <i>ideality</i>, by contrast, are two disjunctive categories: every entity is either real or ideal. Ideal entities are universal, returnable and always existing while real entities are individual, unique and destructible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cicovacki_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cicovacki-166">[165]</a></sup> Among the ideal entities are mathematical objects and values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> The <i>modalities of being</i> are divided into the absolute modalities (actuality and non-actuality) and the relative modalities (possibility, impossibility and necessity). The relative modalities are <i>relative</i> in the sense that they depend on the absolute modalities: something is possible, impossible or necessary because something else is actual. Hartmann asserts that reality is made up of four <a href="/wiki/Integrative_level" title="Integrative level">levels</a> (<i>inanimate</i>, <i>biological</i>, <i>psychological</i> and <i>spiritual</i>) that form a hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poli_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poli-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartmann_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carnap">Carnap</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Carnap">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap" title="Rudolf Carnap">Rudolf Carnap</a> proposed that the truth value of ontological statements about the existence of entities depends on the linguistic framework in which these statements are made: they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal-external_distinction" title="Internal-external distinction">internal</a> to the framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofweber_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hofweber-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahlstrom_116-6"><a href="#cite_note-Dahlstrom-116">[115]</a></sup> As such, they are often trivial in that it just depends on the rules and definitions within this framework. For example, it follows analytically from the rules and definitions within the mathematical framework that numbers exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leitgeb_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leitgeb-168">[167]</a></sup> The problem Carnap saw with traditional ontologists is that they try to make framework-independent or <i>external</i> statements about what <i>really</i> is the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandkühler_127-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sandkühler-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> Such statements are at best <i>pragmatic considerations</i> about which framework to choose and at worst outright <i>meaningless</i>, according to Carnap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> For example, there is no matter of fact as to whether <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Realism_(philosophical)" title="Realism (philosophical)">realism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> is true, their truth depends on the adopted framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> The job of philosophers is not to discover which things exist by themselves but "conceptual engineering": to create interesting frameworks and to explore the consequences of adopting them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leitgeb_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leitgeb-168">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofweber_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hofweber-1">[1]</a></sup> The choice of framework is guided by practical considerations like expedience or fruitfulness since there is no framework-independent notion of truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quine">Quine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Quine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The notion of <a href="/wiki/Ontological_commitment" title="Ontological commitment">ontological commitment</a> plays a central role in <a href="/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine" title="Willard Van Orman Quine">Willard Van Orman Quine</a>'s contributions to ontology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> A theory is ontologically committed to an entity if that entity must exist in order for the theory to be true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bricker_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bricker-175">[174]</a></sup> Quine proposed that the best way to determine this is by translating the theory in question into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First-order_predicate_logic" title="First-order predicate logic">first-order predicate logic</a>. Of special interest in this translation are the logical constants known as <a href="/wiki/Existential_quantification" title="Existential quantification">existential quantifiers</a>, whose meaning corresponds to expressions like "there exists..." or "for some...". They are used to <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic#Free_and_bound_variables" title="First-order logic">bind the variables</a> in the expression following the quantifier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> The ontological commitments of the theory then correspond to the variables bound by existential quantifiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> This approach is summed up by Quine's famous dictum that "[t]o be is to be the value of a variable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quine_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quine-178">[177]</a></sup> This method by itself is not sufficient for ontology since it depends on a theory in order to result in ontological commitments. Quine proposed that we should base our ontology on our best scientific theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bricker_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bricker-175">[174]</a></sup> Various followers of Quine's method chose to apply it to different fields, for example to "everyday conceptions expressed in natural language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ontological_topics">Other ontological topics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Other ontological topics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Ontology%22" rel="nofollow">"Ontology"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Ontology%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Ontology%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Ontology%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Ontology%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Ontology%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontological_formations">Ontological formations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Ontological formations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The concept of <i>ontological formations</i> refers to formations of social relations understood as dominant ways of living. Temporal, spatial, corporeal, <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological</a>, and performative relations are taken to be central to understanding a dominant formation. That is, a particular ontological formation is based on how ontological categories of time, space, embodiment, knowing and performing are lived—objectively and subjectively. Different ontological formations include the <i>customary</i> (including the tribal), the <i>traditional</i>, the <i>modern</i>, and the <i>postmodern</i>. The concept was first introduced by <a href="/wiki/Paul_James_(academic)" title="Paul James (academic)">Paul James</a> in 2006, together with a series of writers including Damian Grenfell and <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Steger" title="Manfred Steger">Manfred Steger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Engaged_theory" title="Engaged theory">engaged theory</a></i> approach, ontological formations are seen as layered and intersecting rather than singular formations. They are 'formations of being'. This approach avoids the usual problems of a Great Divide being posited between the modern and the pre-modern. From a philosophical distinction concerning different formations of being, the concept then provides a way of translating into practical understandings concerning how humans might design cities and communities that live creatively across different ontological formations, for example cities that are not completely dominated by modern valences of spatial configuration. Here the work of Tony Fry is important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontology_of_fictional_characters">Ontology of fictional characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Ontology of fictional characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Edward_N._Zalta" title="Edward N. Zalta">Edward N. Zalta</a>, the ontology of fiction analyses such sentences as:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>'<a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> worshipped (the god) <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a>;'</li>
<li>'<a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a>, the god, does not exist;' and</li>
<li>'<a href="/wiki/Eliza_Doolittle" title="Eliza Doolittle">Eliza Doolittle</a>, in <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Pygmalion_(play)" title="Pygmalion (play)"><i>Pygmalion</i></a>, is a flower girl.'</li></ul>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amie_L._Thomasson" title="Amie L. Thomasson">Amie L. Thomasson</a>, fictional discourse can be of four sorts:
</p>
<ul><li>Uttered <i>within</i> works of fiction;</li>
<li>Philosophical exercises such as '<a href="/wiki/Captain_Marvel_(DC_Comics)" title="Captain Marvel (DC Comics)">Captain Marvel</a> does not exist';</li>
<li>Treating fictional characters as if they were 'real', such as '<a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> can leap tall buildings;' and</li>
<li>Discourse <i>about</i> works of fiction, such as '<a href="/wiki/Pygmalion_(play)" title="Pygmalion (play)">Professor Higgins</a> was created by <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomasson_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomasson-184">[183]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a> distinguished three kinds of entities:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the <i>real</i>: those that can be perceived, or can be inferred from perception.</li>
<li>the <i>fictitious</i>: abstractions that referred to perceptible things.</li>
<li>the <i>fabulous</i>: those that can be found only in the imagination, where the word 'exist' applies to such only in the sense that they do not really exist.</li></ul>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francis_Herbert_Bradley" title="Francis Herbert Bradley">Francis Herbert Bradley</a> thought that real things exist respectively at particular times and places. He recognised several kinds of entity:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the genuinely historical;</li>
<li>the fictional;</li>
<li>the real;</li>
<li>the merely imagined;</li>
<li>the existent; and</li>
<li>the non-existent.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexius_Meinong" title="Alexius Meinong">Alexius Meinong</a> would put fictional entities into the category which he called <i>subsistence</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-187">[186]</a></sup> This category contains objects that neither exist spatially or non-spatially. However, they do have properties. The properties are given to these objects in the way they are said to be described. For example, we can talk about the tall unicorn even though the tall unicorn does not exist. We can say the unicorn is in fact tall because this follows from the properties in which the object is characterized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-187">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontological_and_epistemological_certainty">Ontological and epistemological certainty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Ontological and epistemological certainty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, with <i><a href="/wiki/Cogito,_ergo_sum" title="Cogito, ergo sum">cogito, ergo sum</a></i> (<i>je pense donc je suis,</i> "I think, therefore I am"), argued that a person's thinking agency, his <i>res cogitans</i>, as distinct from his material body, his <i>res extensa</i>, is something that we can know exists with <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological</a> certainty. Descartes argued further that this knowledge could lead to a proof of the certainty of the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>, using the <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">ontological argument</a> that had been formulated first by <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Body_and_environment.2C_questioning_the_meaning_of_being"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Body_and_environment,_questioning_the_meaning_of_being">Body and environment, questioning the meaning of being</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Body and environment, questioning the meaning of being">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Schools of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical_subjectivism" title="Metaphysical subjectivism">subjectivism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical_objectivism" title="Metaphysical objectivism">objectivism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Relativism" title="Relativism">relativism</a> existed at various times in the 20th century, and the <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embodied_philosophy" title="Embodied philosophy">body philosophers</a> tried to reframe all these questions in terms of bodies taking some specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_action" title="Philosophy of action">action</a> in an environment. This relied to a great degree on insights derived from scientific research into animals taking instinctive action in natural and artificial settings—as studied by <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The processes by which bodies related to environments became of great concern, and the idea of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">being</a> itself became difficult to really define. What did people mean when they said "A is B", "A must be B", "A was B"...? Some linguists advocated dropping the verb "to be" from the English language, leaving "<a href="/wiki/E-Prime" title="E-Prime">E Prime</a>", supposedly less prone to bad abstractions. Others, mostly philosophers, tried to dig into the word and its usage. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a> distinguished <i>human being</i> as <i>existence</i> from the being of things in the world. Heidegger proposes that our way of being human and the way the world is for us are cast historically through a fundamental ontological questioning. These fundamental ontological categories provide the basis for communication in an age: a horizon of unspoken and seemingly unquestionable background meanings, such as human beings understood unquestioningly as subjects and other entities understood unquestioningly as objects. Because these basic ontological meanings both generate and are regenerated in everyday interactions, the locus of our way of being in a historical epoch is the communicative event of language in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde-190">[189]</a></sup> For Heidegger, however, communication in the <i>first</i> place is not among human beings, but language itself shapes up in response to questioning (the inexhaustible meaning of) being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> Even the focus of traditional ontology on the 'whatness' or <i>quidditas</i> of beings in their substantial, standing presence can be shifted to pose the question of the 'whoness' of human being itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontology_and_language">Ontology and language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Ontology and language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some philosophers suggest that the question of "What is?" is (at least in part) an issue of <i>usage</i> rather than a question about facts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup> This perspective is conveyed by an analogy made by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Davidson_(philosopher)" title="Donald Davidson (philosopher)">Donald Davidson</a>: Suppose a person refers to a 'cup' as a 'chair' and makes some comments pertinent to a cup, but uses the word 'chair' consistently throughout instead of 'cup'. One might readily catch on that this person simply calls a 'cup' a 'chair' and the oddity is explained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-194">[193]</a></sup> Analogously, if we find people asserting 'there are' such-and-such, and we do not ourselves think that 'such-and-such' exist, we might conclude that these people are not nuts (Davidson calls this assumption 'charity'), they simply use 'there are' differently than we do. The question of <i>What is?</i> is at least partly a topic in the philosophy of language, and is not entirely about ontology itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krieger_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krieger-195">[194]</a></sup> This viewpoint has been expressed by <a href="/wiki/Eli_Hirsch" title="Eli Hirsch">Eli Hirsch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirsch_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirsch-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirsch1_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirsch1-197">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hirsch interprets <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a> as asserting that different concepts of "the existence of something" can be correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirsch1_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hirsch1-197">[196]</a></sup> This position does not contradict the view that some things do exist, but points out that different 'languages' will have different rules about assigning this property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirsch1_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hirsch1-197">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hirsch2_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hirsch2-198">[197]</a></sup> How to determine the 'fitness' of a 'language' to the world then becomes a subject for investigation.
</p><p>Common to all <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_copula" title="Indo-European copula">Indo-European copula</a> languages is the double use of the verb "to be" in both stating that entity X exists ("X is.") as well as stating that X has a property ("X is P"). It is sometimes argued that a third use is also distinct, stating that X is a member of a class ("X is a C"). In other language families these roles may have completely different verbs and are less likely to be confused with one another. For example they might say something like "the car has redness" rather than "the car is red." Hence any discussion of "being" in Indo-European language philosophy may need to make distinctions between these senses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontology_and_human_geography">Ontology and human geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Ontology and human geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In human geography there are two types of ontology: small "o" which accounts for the practical orientation, describing functions of being a part of the group, thought to oversimplify and ignore key activities. The other "o", or big "O", systematically, logically, and rationally describes the essential characteristics and universal traits. This concept relates closely to Plato's view that the human mind can only perceive a bigger world if they continue to live within the confines of their "caves". However, in spite of the differences, ontology relies on the symbolic agreements among members. That said, ontology is crucial for the axiomatic language frameworks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropology">Anthropology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Anthropology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The topic of ontology has received increased attention in <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> since the 1990s. This is sometimes termed the "<a href="/wiki/Ontological_turn" title="Ontological turn">ontological turn</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2013_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2013-200">[199]</a></sup> This type of inquiry is focused on how people from different cultures experience and understand the nature of being. Specific interest in this regard has been given to the ontological outlook of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_people" title="Indigenous people">indigenous people</a> and how their outlook tends to differ from a more Western perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2013_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2013-200">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heywood2012_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heywood2012-201">[200]</a></sup> As an example of this contrast, it has been argued that various indigenous communities ascribe <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a> to non-human entities, like plants, forests, or rivers. This outlook is known as <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reality_and_actuality">Reality and actuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Reality and actuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred N. Whitehead</a>, for ontology, it is useful to distinguish the terms 'reality' and 'actuality'. In this view, an 'actual entity' has a philosophical status of fundamental ontological priority, while a 'real entity' is one which may be actual, or may derive its reality from its logical relation to some actual entity or entities. For example, an occasion in the life of Socrates is an actual entity. But Socrates' being a man does not make 'man' an actual entity, because it refers indeterminately to many actual entities, such as several occasions in the life of Socrates, and also to several occasions in the lives of <a href="/wiki/Alcibiades" title="Alcibiades">Alcibiades</a>, and of others. But the notion of man is real; it derives its reality from its reference to those many actual occasions, each of which is an actual entity. An actual occasion is a concrete entity, while terms such as 'man' are abstractions from many concrete relevant entities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>According to Whitehead, an actual entity must earn its philosophical status of fundamental ontological priority by satisfying several philosophical criteria, as follows:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>There is no going behind an actual entity, to find something more fundamental in fact or in efficacy. This criterion is to be regarded as expressing an axiom, or postulated distinguished doctrine.</li>
<li>An actual entity must be completely determinate in the sense that there may be no confusion about its identity that would allow it to be confounded with another actual entity. In this sense an actual entity is completely concrete, with no potential to be something other than itself. It is what it is. It is a source of potentiality for the creation of other actual entities, of which it may be said to be a part cause. Likewise it is the concretion or realization of potentialities of other actual entities which are its partial causes.</li>
<li>Causation between actual entities is essential to their actuality. Consequently, for Whitehead, each actual entity has its distinct and definite extension in physical <a href="/wiki/Minkowski_space" title="Minkowski space">Minkowski space</a>, and so is uniquely identifiable. A description in Minkowski space supports descriptions in time and space for particular observers.</li>
<li>It is part of the aim of the philosophy of such an ontology as Whitehead's that the actual entities should be all alike, <i>qua</i> actual entities; they should all satisfy a single definite set of well stated ontological criteria of actuality.</li></ul>
<p>Whitehead proposed that his notion of an occasion of experience satisfies the criteria for its status as the philosophically preferred definition of an actual entity. From a purely logical point of view, each occasion of experience has in full measure the characters of both objective and subjective reality. Subjectivity and objectivity refer to different aspects of an occasion of experience, and in no way do they exclude each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of other philosophical proposals or candidates as actual entities, in this view, are Aristotle's 'substances', Leibniz' monads, and Descartes' <i>res verae</i>, and the more modern 'states of affairs'. Aristotle's substances, such as Socrates, have behind them as more fundamental the 'primary substances', and in this sense do not satisfy Whitehead's criteria. Whitehead is not happy with Leibniz' monads as actual entities because they are "windowless" and do not cause each other. 'States of affairs' are often not closely defined, often without specific mention of extension in physical Minkowski space; they are therefore not necessarily processes of becoming, but may be as their name suggests, simply static states in some sense. States of affairs are contingent on particulars, and therefore have something behind them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> One summary of the Whiteheadian actual entity is that it is a process of becoming. Another summary, referring to its causal linkage to other actual entities, is that it is "all window", in contrast with Leibniz' windowless monads.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>This view allows philosophical entities other than actual entities to really exist, but not as fundamentally and primarily factual or causally efficacious; they have existence as abstractions, with reality only derived from their reference to actual entities. A Whiteheadian actual entity has a unique and completely definite place and time. Whiteheadian abstractions are not so tightly defined in time and place, and in the extreme, some are timeless and placeless, or 'eternal' entities. All abstractions have logical or conceptual rather than efficacious existence; their lack of definite time does not make them unreal if they refer to actual entities. Whitehead calls this 'the ontological principle'.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microcosmic_ontology">Microcosmic ontology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Microcosmic ontology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is an established and long philosophical history of the concept of atoms as microscopic physical objects. They are far too small to be visible to the naked eye. It was as recent as the nineteenth century that precise estimates of the sizes of putative physical <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> began to become plausible. Almost direct empirical observation of atomic effects was due to the theoretical investigation of <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a> by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> in the very early twentieth century. But even then, the real existence of atoms was debated by some. Such debate might be labeled 'microcosmic ontology'. Here the word 'microcosm' is used to indicate a physical world of small entities, such as for example atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>Subatomic particles are usually considered to be much smaller than atoms. Their real or actual existence may be very difficult to demonstrate empirically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> A distinction is sometimes drawn between actual and <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual</a> subatomic particles. Reasonably, one may ask, in what sense, if any, do virtual particles exist as physical entities? For atomic and subatomic particles, difficult questions arise, such as do they possess a precise position, or a precise momentum? A question that continues to be controversial is "to what kind of physical thing, if any, does the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> refer?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isham_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Isham-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontological_argument">Ontological argument</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Ontological argument">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">Ontological argument</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosophy" title="Christian philosophy">Western Christian</a> tradition, in his 1078 work <i><a href="/wiki/Proslogion" title="Proslogion">Proslogion</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a> proposed what is known as 'the ontological argument' for the existence of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[note 2]</a></sup> Anselm defined God as "that than which nothing greater can be thought", and argued that this being must exist in the mind, even in the mind of the person who denies the existence of God. He suggested that, if the greatest possible being exists in the mind, it must also exist in reality. If it only exists in the mind, then an even greater being must be possible—one which exists both in the mind and in reality. Therefore, this greatest possible being must exist in reality. Seventeenth century French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> deployed a similar argument. Descartes published several variations of his argument, each of which centred on the idea that God's existence is immediately inferable from a "clear and distinct" idea of a supremely perfect being. In the early eighteenth century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> augmented Descartes's ideas in an attempt to prove that a "supremely perfect" being is a coherent concept. <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a> revived the ontological argument in 1960 when he located a second, stronger ontological argument in Anselm's work; <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a> challenged this argument and proposed an alternative, based on <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a>. Attempts have also been made to validate Anselm's proof using an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automated_theorem_prover" title="Automated theorem prover">automated theorem prover</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>More recently, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del" title="Kurt Gödel">Kurt Gödel</a> proposed a <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">formal argument</a> <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_ontological_proof" title="Gödel's ontological proof">for God's existence</a>. Other arguments for God's existence have been advanced, including those made by Islamic philosophers <a href="/wiki/Mulla_Sadra" title="Mulla Sadra">Mulla Sadra</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allama_Tabatabai" title="Allama Tabatabai">Allama Tabatabai</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hintikka.27s_locution_for_existence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hintikka's_locution_for_existence">Hintikka's locution for existence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Hintikka's locution for existence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jaakko_Hintikka" title="Jaakko Hintikka">Jaakko Hintikka</a> puts the view that a useful explication of the notion of existence is in the words "one can find," implicitly in some world or <a href="/wiki/Domain_of_discourse" title="Domain of discourse">universe of discourse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ontology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">ὄν is the <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present-tense</a> <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participle</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verb</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B5%E1%BC%B0%CE%BC%CE%AF" title="wikt:εἰμί">εἰμί</a> (<i>eimí</i>, 'to be' or 'I am').</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-208">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"There are three main periods in the history of ontological arguments. The first was in 11th century, when St. Anselm of Canterbury came up with the first ontological argument."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

Pp. 68–95 in <i>Quantifier Variance and Realism: Essays in Metaontology</i>. First published as "<a class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1758-2237.2002.tb00061.x/abstract" rel="nofollow">Quantifier variance and realism</a>."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Hirsch2-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hirsch2_198-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHirsch,_Eli2004">Hirsch, Eli (2004). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Moz8OeX4w8AC&amp;pg=PA224" rel="nofollow">"Sosa's Existential Relativism"</a>.  In John Greco (ed.). <i>Ernest Sosa and His Critics</i>. Blackwell. pp. 224–232. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-470-75547-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-470-75547-1"><bdi>978-0-470-75547-1</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Sosa%27s+Existential+Relativism&amp;rft.btitle=Ernest+Sosa+and+His+Critics&amp;rft.pages=224-232&amp;rft.pub=Blackwell&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-470-75547-1&amp;rft.au=Hirsch%2C+Eli&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DMoz8OeX4w8AC%26pg%3DPA224&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOntology"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-199"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-199">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Harvey, F. 2006. "Ontology. Pp. 341–43 in <i>Encyclopedia of Human Geography</i>, edited by B. Warf. Thousand Oaks, CA: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SAGE_Publications" title="SAGE Publications">SAGE Publications</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Scott2013-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Scott2013_200-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Scott2013_200-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFScott2013">Scott, Michael W. (2013). <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/42001687" rel="nofollow">"The anthropology of ontology (religious science?)"</a>. <i>The Journal of the Royal Anthropological Institute</i>. <b>19</b> (4): 859–872. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2F1467-9655.12067" rel="nofollow">10.1111/1467-9655.12067</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/42001687" rel="nofollow">42001687</a>. <q>Since roughly the 1990s, a growing number of anthropologists have become interested in the study of ontology – the investigation and theorization of diverse experiences and understandings of the nature of being itself. This generally takes the form of ethnographic accounts of indigenous non-Western modes and models of being, presented in more or less explicit contrast with aspects of a Euro-American or modern ontology imputed to conventional anthropology.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Journal+of+the+Royal+Anthropological+Institute&amp;rft.atitle=The+anthropology+of+ontology+%28religious+science%3F%29&amp;rft.volume=19&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=859-872&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2F1467-9655.12067&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F42001687%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.aulast=Scott&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael+W.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F42001687&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOntology"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Heywood2012-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Heywood2012_201-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHeywood2012">Heywood, Paolo (2012). <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/43610895" rel="nofollow">"Anthropology and What There Is: Reflections on 'Ontology'<span class="cs1-kern-right"></span>"</a>. <i>The Cambridge Journal of Anthropology</i>. <b>30</b> (1): 143–151. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3167%2Fca.2012.300112" rel="nofollow">10.3167/ca.2012.300112</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0305-7674" rel="nofollow">0305-7674</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/43610895" rel="nofollow">43610895</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Cambridge+Journal+of+Anthropology&amp;rft.atitle=Anthropology+and+What+There+Is%3A+Reflections+on+%27Ontology%27&amp;rft.volume=30&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=143-151&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.issn=0305-7674&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F43610895%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3167%2Fca.2012.300112&amp;rft.aulast=Heywood&amp;rft.aufirst=Paolo&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F43610895&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOntology"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFLudwigWeiskopf2019">Ludwig, David; Weiskopf, Daniel A. (September 2019). "Ethnoontology: Ways of world‐building across cultures". <i>Philosophy Compass</i>. <b>14</b> (9). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fphc3.12621" rel="nofollow">10.1111/phc3.12621</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:199516840" rel="nofollow">199516840</a>. <q>Consider the animism debate. Animists consider nonhuman entities (e.g., plants, forests, or rivers) as intentional actors (Harvey, 2005). There is substantial evidence that animism is a widespread metaphysical view. For example, the Nayaka people of South India consider not only certain animals but also stones, hills, cups, and knives to be devaru: beings that stand in active, quasi-social relationships with them (Bird-David, 1999). Devaru are aspects of a larger kin structure that incorporates potential “partners” in the nonhuman world. In addition to these ethnographic observations, there are intriguing cross-cultural similarities in animist ontologies. Indigenous communities around the world tend to be much more permissive in their ascription of intentionality than Western participants (Ojalehto, Douglas, &amp; García, 2017).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Philosophy+Compass&amp;rft.atitle=Ethnoontology%3A+Ways+of+world%E2%80%90building+across+cultures&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.issue=9&amp;rft.date=2019-09&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fphc3.12621&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A199516840%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Ludwig&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.au=Weiskopf%2C+Daniel+A.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOntology"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Whitehead, Alfred N.</a> 1929. <i>Process and Reality</i>. Cambridge: <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a>. <i>passim</i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/David_Malet_Armstrong" title="David Malet Armstrong">Armstrong, D.M.</a> (1997). <i>A World of States of Affairs</i>, Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-58064-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-58064-1">0-521-58064-1</a>, p. 1.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-205"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-205">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/microcosm" rel="nofollow">"Definition of MICROCOSM"</a>. <i>www.merriam-webster.com</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2022-09-13</span></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.merriam-webster.com&amp;rft.atitle=Definition+of+MICROCOSM&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.merriam-webster.com%2Fdictionary%2Fmicrocosm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOntology"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-206"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-206">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kaiser, D. 1994. "Niels Bohr's legacy in contemporary particle physics." Pp. 257–268 in <i>Niels Bohr and Contemporary Philosophy</i>, edited by J. Faye and H. J. Folse. Dordrecht, HL: <a href="/wiki/Springer_Publishing" title="Springer Publishing">Springer</a>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-90-481-4299-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-90-481-4299-6">978-90-481-4299-6</a>.

s. 4, ("Questions of ontology and particle physics phenomenology"). pp. 262–64.</span>
</li>

</li>
